	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Left- and right-hand traffic</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Left Hand Drive" redirects here. For the album by the Angels, see <a href="/wiki/Left_Hand_Drive_(album)" title="Left Hand Drive (album)">Left Hand Drive (album)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">directionality of traffic flow by jurisdiction</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Countries_driving_on_the_left_or_right.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Countries_driving_on_the_left_or_right.svg/400px-Countries_driving_on_the_left_or_right.svg.png" decoding="async" width="400" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Countries_driving_on_the_left_or_right.svg/600px-Countries_driving_on_the_left_or_right.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Countries_driving_on_the_left_or_right.svg/800px-Countries_driving_on_the_left_or_right.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2754" data-file-height="1398" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Countries_driving_on_the_left_or_right.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Countries by handedness of road traffic, c. 2019 <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #204A87; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Left-hand traffic</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #CC0000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Right-hand traffic</div></div></div></div>
<p><b>Left-hand traffic</b> (<b>LHT</b>) and <b>right-hand traffic</b> (<b>RHT</b>) are the practice, in <a href="/wiki/Bidirectional_traffic" title="Bidirectional traffic">bidirectional traffic</a>, of keeping to the left side or to the right side of the road, respectively. A fundamental element to <a href="/wiki/Traffic_flow" title="Traffic flow">traffic flow</a>, it is sometimes referred to as the <i>rule of the road</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-kincaid_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kincaid-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>RHT is used in 165 countries and territories, with the remaining 75 countries and territories using LHT.<sup id="cite_ref-chartsbin_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chartsbin-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Countries that use LHT account for about a sixth of the world's area with about a third of its population and a quarter of its roads.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In 1919, 104 of the world's territories were LHT and an equal number were RHT. From 1919 to 1986, 34 of the LHT territories switched to RHT.<sup id="cite_ref-watson_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-watson-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many LHT countries were formerly part of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>, although some were not, such as Japan, Thailand, Indonesia, and Suriname. Conversely, many RHT countries were part of the <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">French colonial empire</a>.
</p><p>For <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport" title="Rail transport">rail transport</a>, LHT predominates in Western Europe (except Germany, Denmark, Austria, Spain, and the Netherlands), Latin America (except <a href="/wiki/Mexico_(country)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mexico (country)">Mexico</a>), and in countries formerly in the British and French Empires, whereas North American and central and eastern European train services operate RHT.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Riverine_Patrol_Boat_Cockpit_Console.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Riverine_Patrol_Boat_Cockpit_Console.jpg/220px-Riverine_Patrol_Boat_Cockpit_Console.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Riverine_Patrol_Boat_Cockpit_Console.jpg/330px-Riverine_Patrol_Boat_Cockpit_Console.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Riverine_Patrol_Boat_Cockpit_Console.jpg/440px-Riverine_Patrol_Boat_Cockpit_Console.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="2248" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Riverine_Patrol_Boat_Cockpit_Console.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Boats are traditionally piloted from <a href="/wiki/Starboard" class="mw-redirect" title="Starboard">starboard</a> to facilitate <a href="/wiki/Priority_to_the_right" title="Priority to the right">priority to the right</a></div></div></div>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Regulations_for_Preventing_Collisions_at_Sea" title="International Regulations for Preventing Collisions at Sea">International Regulations for Preventing Collisions at Sea</a>, water traffic is effectively RHT: a vessel proceeding along a narrow channel must keep to <a href="/wiki/Starboard" class="mw-redirect" title="Starboard">starboard</a> (the right-hand side), and when two power-driven vessels are meeting head-on both must alter course to starboard also. For aircraft the US <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aviation_Regulations" title="Federal Aviation Regulations">Federal Aviation Regulations</a> suggest RHT principles, both in the air and on water.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In LHT vehicles keep left, and cars are <b>RHD</b> (right-hand drive) with the steering wheel on the right-hand side and the driver sitting on the offside or side closest to the center of the road. The passenger sits on the nearside, closest to the curb. <a href="/wiki/Roundabout" title="Roundabout">Roundabouts</a> circulate clockwise. In RHT everything is reversed: cars keep right, the driver sits on the left side of the car, and roundabouts circulate counterclockwise.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-2"><div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Left-_and_right-hand_traffic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Left-_and_right-hand_traffic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Danish_motorcyclists_about_to_cross_the_border_between_Sweden_and_Norway_in_1934.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Danish_motorcyclists_about_to_cross_the_border_between_Sweden_and_Norway_in_1934.png/220px-Danish_motorcyclists_about_to_cross_the_border_between_Sweden_and_Norway_in_1934.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Danish_motorcyclists_about_to_cross_the_border_between_Sweden_and_Norway_in_1934.png/330px-Danish_motorcyclists_about_to_cross_the_border_between_Sweden_and_Norway_in_1934.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Danish_motorcyclists_about_to_cross_the_border_between_Sweden_and_Norway_in_1934.png/440px-Danish_motorcyclists_about_to_cross_the_border_between_Sweden_and_Norway_in_1934.png 2x" data-file-width="3711" data-file-height="2561" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Danish_motorcyclists_about_to_cross_the_border_between_Sweden_and_Norway_in_1934.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Border between Sweden and Norway in 1934</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kungsgatan_1967.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Kungsgatan_1967.jpg/220px-Kungsgatan_1967.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Kungsgatan_1967.jpg/330px-Kungsgatan_1967.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Kungsgatan_1967.jpg/440px-Kungsgatan_1967.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1888" data-file-height="1112" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kungsgatan_1967.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Traffic moves from left to right in <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>, Sweden, on 3 September 1967</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Drive_on_Left_sign,_Dublin_Airport,_August_2019.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/97/Drive_on_Left_sign%2C_Dublin_Airport%2C_August_2019.jpg/220px-Drive_on_Left_sign%2C_Dublin_Airport%2C_August_2019.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/97/Drive_on_Left_sign%2C_Dublin_Airport%2C_August_2019.jpg/330px-Drive_on_Left_sign%2C_Dublin_Airport%2C_August_2019.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/97/Drive_on_Left_sign%2C_Dublin_Airport%2C_August_2019.jpg/440px-Drive_on_Left_sign%2C_Dublin_Airport%2C_August_2019.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1899" data-file-height="1407" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Drive_on_Left_sign,_Dublin_Airport,_August_2019.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sign in <a href="/wiki/Dublin_Airport" title="Dublin Airport">Dublin Airport</a> reminding visiting motorists to drive on the left</div></div></div>
<p>Ancient Greek, Egyptian, and Roman troops kept to the left when marching.<sup id="cite_ref-anderson_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anderson-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In 1998, archaeologists found a well-preserved double track leading to a Roman quarry near <a href="/wiki/Swindon" title="Swindon">Swindon</a>, in southern England. The grooves in the road were much deeper on the left side when facing away from the quarry than those on the right side. This suggests LHT at this location, since carts would exit the quarry heavily loaded and enter it empty.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In the year 1300, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Boniface_VIII" title="Pope Boniface VIII">Pope Boniface VIII</a> directed pilgrims to keep left.<sup id="cite_ref-anderson_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anderson-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first reference in <a href="/wiki/English_law" title="English law">English law</a> to LHT was in 1756, with regard to <a href="/wiki/London_Bridge" title="London Bridge">London Bridge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hamer_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hamer-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, all traffic in France kept right.<sup id="cite_ref-hamer_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hamer-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Rotterdam was LHT until 1917,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> although the rest of the Netherlands was RHT.
</p><p>Russia completely switched to RHT in the last days of the Tsars in February 1917. 
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Austro-Hungarian Empire">Austro-Hungarian Empire</a> broke up, the resulting countries gradually changed to RHT. In <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vorarlberg" title="Vorarlberg">Vorarlberg</a> switched in 1921, <a href="/wiki/North_Tyrol" title="North Tyrol">North Tyrol</a> in 1930, <a href="/wiki/Carinthia" title="Carinthia">Carinthia</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Tyrol" title="East Tyrol">East Tyrol</a> in 1935, and the rest of the country in 1938.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transylvania" title="Transylvania">Transylvania</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Banat" title="Banat">Banat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bukovina" title="Bukovina">Bukovina</a> were LHT until 1919, while <a href="/wiki/Wallachia" title="Wallachia">Wallachia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moldavia" title="Moldavia">Moldavia</a> were already RHT. <a href="/wiki/Partitions_of_Poland" title="Partitions of Poland">Partitions of Poland</a> belonging to the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> were RHT, while the <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Partition" title="Austrian Partition">Austrian Partition</a> changed in the 1920s<sup id="cite_ref-Komunikacja.krakow.eurocity.pl_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Komunikacja.krakow.eurocity.pl-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Croatia-Slavonia switched on joining the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Yugoslavia" title="Kingdom of Yugoslavia">Kingdom of Yugoslavia</a> in 1918, although <a href="/wiki/Istria_County" title="Istria County">Istria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia" title="Dalmatia">Dalmatia</a> were already RHT.<sup id="cite_ref-croatiaslavonia_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-croatiaslavonia-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Nazi Germany introduced the <a href="/wiki/Switch_to_right-hand_traffic_in_Czechoslovakia" title="Switch to right-hand traffic in Czechoslovakia">switch in Czechoslovakia</a> and Slovakia in 1938–1939.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> West Ukraine was LHT, but the rest of Ukraine, having been part of the Russian Empire, was RHT.
</p><p>In Italy, the countryside was RHT while cities were LHT until 1927.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Rome changed to RHT in 1924 and Milan in 1926. However, the <a href="/wiki/Rome_Metro" title="Rome Metro">Rome Metro</a>, built in 1955, uses LHT. <a href="/wiki/Alfa_Romeo" title="Alfa Romeo">Alfa Romeo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lancia" title="Lancia">Lancia</a> produced RHD cars to special order until 1950 and 1953 respectively; many drivers preferred RHD even in RHT, as it offered the driver a clearer view of the edges of mountain roads that lacked barriers.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> switched to RHT in 1928.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, formerly part of LHT Sweden, switched to RHT in 1858 as the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Finland" title="Grand Duchy of Finland">Grand Duchy of Finland</a> by Russian decree.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> switched to RHT in 1967. It was LHT from about 1734<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> despite having land borders with RHT countries, and approximately 90% of cars being left-hand drive (LHD).<sup id="cite_ref-Réalités_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Réalités-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> A referendum in 1955 overwhelmingly rejected a change to RHT but years later the government ordered it, and it occurred on Sunday, 3 September 1967<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> at 5&#160;am. The accident rate then dropped sharply,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> but soon rose to near its original level.<sup id="cite_ref-salon20090814_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-salon20090814-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The day was known as Högertrafikomläggningen or <a href="/wiki/Dagen_H" title="Dagen H">Dagen H</a> for short. When Iceland switched the following year, it was known as <i>Hægri dagurinn</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/H-dagurinn" title="H-dagurinn">H-dagurinn</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Most passenger cars in Iceland were already LHD.
</p><p>The United Kingdom is LHT, but its <a href="/wiki/British_Overseas_Territory" class="mw-redirect" title="British Overseas Territory">overseas territories</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Indian_Ocean_Territory" title="British Indian Ocean Territory">British Indian Ocean Territory</a> are RHT. In the late 1960s, the UK <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Transport" title="Department for Transport">Department for Transport</a> considered switching to RHT, but declared it unsafe and too costly for such a built-up nation.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Road building standards, for motorways in particular, allow asymmetrically designed road junctions, where merge and diverge lanes differ in length.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, four countries in Europe continue to use LHT, all island nations and formerly parts of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>: the UK (including Northern Ireland), Cyprus, Republic of Ireland, and Malta.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Left-_and_right-hand_traffic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SADC_road_sign_R137.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/SADC_road_sign_R137.svg/100px-SADC_road_sign_R137.svg.png" decoding="async" width="100" height="100" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/SADC_road_sign_R137.svg/150px-SADC_road_sign_R137.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/SADC_road_sign_R137.svg/200px-SADC_road_sign_R137.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="909" data-file-height="909" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SADC_road_sign_R137.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Southern_African_Development_Community" title="Southern African Development Community">Southern African Development Community</a> LHT roundabout sign</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SADC_road_sign_R137-RHT.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/SADC_road_sign_R137-RHT.svg/100px-SADC_road_sign_R137-RHT.svg.png" decoding="async" width="100" height="100" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/SADC_road_sign_R137-RHT.svg/150px-SADC_road_sign_R137-RHT.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/SADC_road_sign_R137-RHT.svg/200px-SADC_road_sign_R137-RHT.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="909" data-file-height="909" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SADC_road_sign_R137-RHT.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>RHT roundabout sign</div></div></div>
<p>Egypt was conquered by Napoleon and it kept RHT even after it became a British dependency. 
</p><p>LHT was introduced in <a href="/wiki/British_West_Africa" title="British West Africa">British West Africa</a>. All of the countries formerly part of this colony border with former French RHT jurisdictions and have switched to RHT since decolonization. These include Ghana, Gambia,<sup id="cite_ref-TBD_Gambia_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TBD_Gambia-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Sierra Leone, and Nigeria. Britain introduced LHT to the <a href="/wiki/East_Africa_Protectorate" title="East Africa Protectorate">East Africa Protectorate</a> (now Kenya, Tanzania and Uganda), <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Colony" title="Cape Colony">Cape Colony</a> (now Zambia, Zimbabwe and South Africa). All of these have remained LHT. Sudan, formerly part of <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Egyptian_Sudan" title="Anglo-Egyptian Sudan">Anglo-Egyptian Sudan</a> switched to RHT in 1973, as it is surrounded by neighbouring RHT countries. 
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a>, then LHT, introduced LHT to <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Mozambique" title="Portuguese Mozambique">Portuguese Mozambique</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Angola" title="Portuguese Angola">Portuguese Angola</a>. Although Portugal itself switched to RHT in 1928, these territories remained LHT as they have land borders with former British colonies. Other former Portuguese colonies in Africa including <a href="/wiki/Guinea-Bissau" title="Guinea-Bissau">Guinea-Bissau</a>, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Tom%C3%A9_and_Pr%C3%ADncipe" title="São Tomé and Príncipe">São Tomé and Príncipe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verde" title="Cape Verde">Cape Verde</a> switched to RHT in 1928.
</p><p>France introduced RHT in <a href="/wiki/French_West_Africa" title="French West Africa">French West Africa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>, where it is still used. Countries in this former colony include <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burkina_Faso" title="Burkina Faso">Burkina Faso</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benin" title="Benin">Benin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>. Other French former colonies that are RHT include <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Djibouti" title="Djibouti">Djibouti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gabon" title="Gabon">Gabon</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Republic of the Congo">Republic of the Congo</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a>, former Belgian colonies in <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa" title="Central Africa">Central Africa</a>, are RHT but are considering switching to LHT<sup id="cite_ref-rwandaburundi_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rwandaburundi-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rwandatransport_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rwandatransport-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> like neighbouring members of the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Community" title="East African Community">East African Community</a> (EAC).<sup id="cite_ref-independent1_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent1-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> A survey in 2009 found that 54% of Rwandans favoured the switch. Reasons cited were the perceived lower costs of RHD vehicles, easier maintenance and the political benefit of harmonious traffic regulations with other EAC countries. The survey indicated that RHD cars were 16% to 49% cheaper than their LHD counterparts.<sup id="cite_ref-business_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-business-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> In 2014, an internal report by consultants to the Ministry of Infrastructure recommended a switch to LHT.<sup id="cite_ref-ETRR_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ETRR-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, the ban on RHD vehicles was lifted; RHD trucks from neighbouring countries cost $1000 less than LHD models imported from Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Left-_and_right-hand_traffic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nb-stj-1899.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Nb-stj-1899.jpg/220px-Nb-stj-1899.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Nb-stj-1899.jpg/330px-Nb-stj-1899.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Nb-stj-1899.jpg/440px-Nb-stj-1899.jpg 2x" data-file-width="641" data-file-height="481" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nb-stj-1899.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Saint John, New Brunswick, circa 1898. Parts of Canada were LHT until the 1920s.</div></div></div>
<p>In what is now Canada, LHT was introduced by the British in <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a>, which changed to RHT in stages from 1920 to 1923,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/New_Brunswick" title="New Brunswick">New Brunswick</a>, Nova Scotia, and <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward_Island" title="Prince Edward Island">Prince Edward Island</a>, which changed in 1922, 1923, and 1924 respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-ns1758.ca_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ns1758.ca-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland</a>, then a British colony,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> changed to RHT in 1947, two years before joining Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-A_triumph_for_left_over_right_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_triumph_for_left_over_right-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Former parts of <a href="/wiki/New_France" title="New France">New France</a> have always been RHT.<sup id="cite_ref-cbc_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cbc-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In the early years of British colonisation of North America in 18th century, British driving customs were followed and the original <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">Thirteen Colonies</a> drove on the left. After declaring independence from the United Kingdom in 4 July 1776, however, they were anxious to cast off all remaining links with their British colonial past and gradually changed to right-hand driving, influenced by a number of factors, including gratitude for French help in the War of Independence, the views of those Americans with roots in continental Europe and specifically the influence of General Lafayette, the French liberal reformer. Incidentally, the influence of other European immigrants, especially the French, should not be underestimated.
</p><p>In the late 1700s, traffic in the United States was RHT based on <a href="/wiki/Teamster" title="Teamster">teamsters</a>' use of large freight wagons pulled by several pairs of horses. The wagons had no driver's seat, so the (typically right-handed) <a href="/wiki/Postilion" title="Postilion">postilion</a> held his whip in his right hand and thus sat on the left rear horse. Seated on the left, the driver preferred that other wagons pass him on the left so that he could be sure to keep clear of the wheels of oncoming wagons.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The first keep-right law for <a href="/wiki/Driving_in_the_United_States" title="Driving in the United States">driving in the United States</a> was passed in 1792 and applied to the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_and_Lancaster_Turnpike" title="Philadelphia and Lancaster Turnpike">Philadelphia and Lancaster Turnpike</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-On_The_Right_Side_of_the_Road_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-On_The_Right_Side_of_the_Road-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> formalized RHT in 1804, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> in 1813 and <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a> in 1821.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Today the United States is RHT except the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Virgin_Islands" title="United States Virgin Islands">United States Virgin Islands</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> which is LHT like many neighboring islands.
</p><p>Some postal service vehicles, garbage trucks, many parking enforcement vehicles and uncommon specialty vehicles in the United States are still being RHD.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/West_Indies" title="West Indies">West Indies</a>, colonies and territories drive on the same side as their parent countries, except for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Virgin_Islands" title="United States Virgin Islands">United States Virgin Islands</a>. Many of the island nations are former British colonies and drive on the left, including <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antigua_and_Barbuda" title="Antigua and Barbuda">Antigua and Barbuda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dominica" title="Dominica">Dominica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grenada" title="Grenada">Grenada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Kitts_and_Nevis" title="Saint Kitts and Nevis">Saint Kitts and Nevis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Lucia" title="Saint Lucia">Saint Lucia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Vincent_and_the_Grenadines" title="Saint Vincent and the Grenadines">Saint Vincent and the Grenadines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">Trinidad and Tobago</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Bahamas" title="The Bahamas">The Bahamas</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Left-_and_right-hand_traffic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lotus-bridge-macau.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Lotus-bridge-macau.jpg/220px-Lotus-bridge-macau.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Lotus-bridge-macau.jpg/330px-Lotus-bridge-macau.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Lotus-bridge-macau.jpg/440px-Lotus-bridge-macau.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1186" data-file-height="666" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lotus-bridge-macau.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vehicles entering and leaving Macau cross over each other at the <a href="/wiki/Lotus_Bridge" class="mw-redirect" title="Lotus Bridge">Lotus Bridge</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>LHT was introduced by the British in <a href="/wiki/Presidencies_and_provinces_of_British_India" title="Presidencies and provinces of British India">British India</a> (now <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>), <a href="/wiki/British_Malaya" title="British Malaya">British Malaya</a> (now Malaysia, Brunei and Singapore), and <a href="/wiki/British_Hong_Kong" title="British Hong Kong">British Hong Kong</a>. All are still LHT except Myanmar, which switched to RHT in 1970,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> although much of its infrastructure still geared to LHT. Most cars are used RHD vehicles imported from Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> was LHT until the 1950s, in line with neighbouring British India and later Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>LHT was introduced by the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a> in <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Macau" title="Portuguese Macau">Portuguese Macau</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Timor" title="Portuguese Timor">Portuguese Timor</a> (now <a href="/wiki/East_Timor" title="East Timor">East Timor</a>). Both places are still LHT, despite Macau now being part of RHT <a href="/wiki/People%E2%80%99s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People’s Republic of China">China</a>, requiring a right-to-left switching interchange at the Lotus Bridge which connects the two. East Timor shares the island of <a href="/wiki/Timor" title="Timor">Timor</a> with Indonesia, which is also LHT, although the former (then Portuguese Timor) switched to RHT along with Portugal in 1928<sup id="cite_ref-kincaid_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kincaid-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> before changing back to LHT in 1976 during the <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_occupation_of_East_Timor" title="Indonesian occupation of East Timor">Indonesian occupation of East Timor</a>.
</p><p>China is RHT except the <a href="/wiki/Special_Administrative_Region_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Special Administrative Region of the People&#39;s Republic of China">Special Administrative Regions</a> of Hong Kong and Macau. LHT was uniform in the 1930s, then the northern provinces were RHT. <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%931949)" title="Republic of China (1912–1949)">Nationalist China</a> adopted RHT in 1946. This convention was preserved when the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_China" title="Communist Party of China">CCP</a> took the mainland and the <a href="/wiki/Kuomintang" title="Kuomintang">KMT</a> retreated to <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>.
</p><p>Both <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> switched to RHT in 1945 after liberation from Japanese colonial power.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> was mostly LHT during its <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Philippines_(1521%E2%80%931898)" title="History of the Philippines (1521–1898)">Spanish</a><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Philippines_(1898%E2%80%931946)" title="History of the Philippines (1898–1946)">American</a> colonial periods,<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> as well as during the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_the_Philippines" title="Commonwealth of the Philippines">Commonwealth era</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_occupation_of_the_Philippines" title="Japanese occupation of the Philippines">Japanese occupation</a>, the Philippines remained LHT,<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> also because LHT had been required by the Japanese;<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> but during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Manila_(1945)" title="Battle of Manila (1945)">Battle of Manila</a>, the liberating American forces drove their tanks to the right for easier facilitation of movement. RHT was formalised in 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-E.O._34_1945_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-E.O._34_1945-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Japan was never part of the British Empire, but its traffic also goes to the left. Although the origin of this habit goes back to the Edo period (1603-1868), it was not until 1872 that this unwritten rule became more or less official. That was the year when Japan’s first railway was introduced, built with technical aid from the British. Gradually, a massive network of railways and tram tracks was built, and of course all trains and trams drove on the left-hand side. Still, it took another half century till in 1924 left-side driving was clearly written in a law.
In <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, Post-World War II <a href="/wiki/Okinawa" class="mw-redirect" title="Okinawa">Okinawa</a> was ruled by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Civil_Administration_of_the_Ryukyu_Islands" title="United States Civil Administration of the Ryukyu Islands">United States Civil Administration of the Ryukyu Islands</a> and was RHT. It was returned to Japan in 1972 but did not convert back to LHT until 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The conversion operation was known as <a href="/wiki/730_(transport)" title="730 (transport)">730</a> (<i>Nana-San-Maru</i>, which refers to the date of the changeover, 30 July). Okinawa is one of few places to have changed from RHT to LHT in the late 1900s.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> became RHT as part of <a href="/wiki/French_Indochina" title="French Indochina">French Indochina</a>, as did <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>. In the latter country, RHD cars, many of which were smuggled from Thailand, were banned from 2001, even though they accounted for 80% of vehicles in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania">Oceania</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Left-_and_right-hand_traffic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Oceania">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Drive_on_left_in_australia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Drive_on_left_in_australia.jpg/100px-Drive_on_left_in_australia.jpg" decoding="async" width="100" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Drive_on_left_in_australia.jpg/150px-Drive_on_left_in_australia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Drive_on_left_in_australia.jpg/200px-Drive_on_left_in_australia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Drive_on_left_in_australia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sign reminding motorists to keep left in Australia.</div></div></div>
<p>Many former British colonies in the region have always been LHT, including <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fiji" title="Fiji">Fiji</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kiribati" title="Kiribati">Kiribati</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands" title="Solomon Islands">Solomon Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tonga" title="Tonga">Tonga</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tuvalu" title="Tuvalu">Tuvalu</a>, as well as nations which were previously administered by Australia, being <a href="/wiki/Nauru" title="Nauru">Nauru</a> and <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Samoa" title="Samoa">Samoa</a>, a former German colony, had been RHT for more than a century. It switched to LHT in 2009,<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Samoa_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Samoa-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> being the first territory in almost 30 years to switch.<sup id="cite_ref-reuters_Samoa_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters_Samoa-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The move was legislated in 2008 to allow Samoans to use cheaper right-hand drive (RHD) vehicles—which are better suited for left-hand traffic—imported from Australia, New Zealand or Japan, and to harmonise with other South Pacific nations. A political party, <a href="/wiki/The_People%27s_Party_(Samoa)" class="mw-redirect" title="The People&#39;s Party (Samoa)">The People's Party</a>, was formed by the group People Against Switching Sides (PASS) to try to protest against the change, with the latter launching a legal challenge,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> and in April 2008 an estimated 18,000 people attended demonstrations against it.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Samoa2_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Samoa2-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The motor industry was also opposed, as 14,000 of Samoa's 18,000 vehicles are designed for RHT and the government has refused to meet the cost of conversion.<sup id="cite_ref-reuters_Samoa_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters_Samoa-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> After months of preparation, the switch from right to left happened in an atmosphere of national celebration. There were no reported incidents.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> At 05:50 local time, Monday 7 September, a radio announcement halted traffic, and an announcement at 6:00 ordered traffic to switch to LHT.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Samoa_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Samoa-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The change coincided with more restrictive enforcement of speeding and seat-belt laws.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> That day and the following day were declared public holidays, to reduce traffic.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The change included a three-day ban on alcohol sales, while police mounted dozens of checkpoints, warning drivers to drive slowly.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_America">South America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Left-_and_right-hand_traffic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: South America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> was a colony of Portugal until the early 19th century and during this century and the early 20th century had mixed rules, with some regions still on LHT, switching these remaining regions to RHT in 1928, the same year Portugal switched sides.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Other Central and South American countries that later switched from LHT to RHT include Argentina, Chile, Panama,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Paraguay,<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> and Uruguay. 
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a>, along with neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">Guyana</a>, are the only two remaining LHT countries in <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-tbr2015_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tbr2015-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Changing_sides_at_borders">Changing sides at borders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Left-_and_right-hand_traffic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Changing sides at borders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Laos_-_Traffic_Switchover_Point.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Laos_-_Traffic_Switchover_Point.svg/100px-Laos_-_Traffic_Switchover_Point.svg.png" decoding="async" width="100" height="88" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Laos_-_Traffic_Switchover_Point.svg/150px-Laos_-_Traffic_Switchover_Point.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Laos_-_Traffic_Switchover_Point.svg/200px-Laos_-_Traffic_Switchover_Point.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="563" data-file-height="497" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Laos_-_Traffic_Switchover_Point.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Traffic Switchover sign at the <a href="/wiki/Thai%E2%80%93Lao_Friendship_Bridge" class="mw-redirect" title="Thai–Lao Friendship Bridge">Thai–Lao Friendship Bridge</a></div></div></div>
<p>Although many LHT jurisdictions are on islands, there are cases where vehicles may be driven from LHT across a border into a RHT area. Such borders are mostly located in Africa and southern Asia. The <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Convention_on_Road_Traffic" title="Vienna Convention on Road Traffic">Vienna Convention on Road Traffic</a> regulates the use of foreign registered vehicles in the 74 countries that have ratified it.
</p><p>LHT Thailand has three RHT neighbors: Cambodia, Laos, and Myanmar. Most of its borders use a simple traffic light to do the switch, but there are also interchanges which enable the switch while keeping up a continuous flow of traffic.<sup id="cite_ref-cnt_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnt-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are four road border crossing points between Hong Kong and Mainland China. In 2006, the daily average number of vehicle trips recorded at Lok Ma Chau was 31,100.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The next largest is <a href="/wiki/Man_Kam_To" title="Man Kam To">Man Kam To</a>, where there is no changeover system and the border roads on the mainland side <a href="/wiki/Wenjindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Wenjindu">Wenjindu</a> intersect as one-way streets with a main road.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Takutu_River_Bridge" title="Takutu River Bridge">Takutu River Bridge</a> (which links LHT Guyana and RHT Brazil<sup id="cite_ref-Takutu_bridge_opens_to_traffic_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Takutu_bridge_opens_to_traffic-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>) is the only border in the Americas where traffic changes sides.
</p><p>Although the United Kingdom is separated from <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">Continental Europe</a> by the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a>, the level of cross-Channel traffic is very high; the <a href="/wiki/Channel_Tunnel" title="Channel Tunnel">Channel Tunnel</a> alone carries 3.5&#160;million vehicles per year by the <a href="/wiki/Eurotunnel_Shuttle" title="Eurotunnel Shuttle">Eurotunnel Shuttle</a> between the UK and France.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Road_vehicle_configurations">Road vehicle configurations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Left-_and_right-hand_traffic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Road vehicle configurations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Driver_seating_position">Driver seating position</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Left-_and_right-hand_traffic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Driver seating position">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In RHT jurisdictions, vehicles are configured with LHD, with the driver sitting on the left side. In LHT jurisdictions, the reverse is true. The driver's side, the side closest to the centre of the road, is sometimes called the <i>offside</i>, while the passenger side, the side closest to the side of the road, is sometimes called the <i>nearside</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most windshield wipers are designed to clear the driver's side better and have a longer blade on the driver's side<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> and wipe up from the passenger side to the driver's side. Thus on LHD configurations, they wipe up from right to left, viewed from inside the vehicle, and do the opposite on RHD vehicles.
</p><p>Historically there was less consistency in the relationship of the position of the driver to the handedness of traffic. Most American cars produced before 1910 were RHD.<sup id="cite_ref-On_The_Right_Side_of_the_Road_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-On_The_Right_Side_of_the_Road-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> In 1908 <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ford" title="Henry Ford">Henry Ford</a> standardised the <a href="/wiki/Model_T" class="mw-redirect" title="Model T">Model T</a> as LHD in RHT America,<sup id="cite_ref-On_The_Right_Side_of_the_Road_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-On_The_Right_Side_of_the_Road-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> arguing that with RHD and RHT, the passenger was obliged to "get out on the street side and walk around the car" and that with steering from the left, the driver "is able to see even the wheels of the other car and easily avoids danger."<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> By 1915 other manufacturers followed Ford's lead, due to the popularity of the Model T.<sup id="cite_ref-On_The_Right_Side_of_the_Road_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-On_The_Right_Side_of_the_Road-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In specialised cases, the driver will sit on the nearside, or kerbside. Examples include:
</p>
<ul><li>Where the driver needs a good view of the nearside, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Street_sweeper" title="Street sweeper">street sweepers</a>, or vehicles driven along unstable road edges.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Where it is more convenient for the driver to be on the nearside, e.g. delivery vehicles. The <a href="/wiki/Grumman_LLV" title="Grumman LLV">Grumman LLV</a> postal delivery truck is widely used with RHD configurations in RHT North America. Some <a href="/wiki/Unimog" title="Unimog">Unimogs</a> are designed to switch between LHD and RHD to permit operators to work on the more convenient side of the truck.</li></ul>
<p>Generally, the convention is to mount a motorcycle on the left,<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Kickstand" title="Kickstand">kickstands</a> are usually on the left<sup id="cite_ref-tips_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tips-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> which makes it more convenient to mount on the safer kerbside<sup id="cite_ref-tips_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tips-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> as is the case in LHT. Some jurisdictions prohibit fitting a <a href="/wiki/Sidecar" title="Sidecar">sidecar</a> to a motorcycle's offside.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Headlamps_and_other_lighting_equipment">Headlamps and other lighting equipment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Left-_and_right-hand_traffic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Headlamps and other lighting equipment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Headlamp" title="Headlamp">Headlamp</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Low_beam_light_pattern_for_right-hand_traffic.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Low_beam_light_pattern_for_right-hand_traffic.svg/220px-Low_beam_light_pattern_for_right-hand_traffic.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="109" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Low_beam_light_pattern_for_right-hand_traffic.svg/330px-Low_beam_light_pattern_for_right-hand_traffic.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Low_beam_light_pattern_for_right-hand_traffic.svg/440px-Low_beam_light_pattern_for_right-hand_traffic.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1130" data-file-height="561" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Low_beam_light_pattern_for_right-hand_traffic.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bird's-eye view of low beam light pattern for RH traffic, with long seeing range on the right and short cutoff on the left so oncoming drivers are not dazzled.</div></div></div>
<p>Most low-beam headlamps produce an asymmetrical light suitable for use on only one side of the road. Low beam headlamps in LHT jurisdictions throw most of their light forward-leftward; those for RHT throw most of their light forward-rightward, thus illuminating obstacles and road signs while minimising glare for oncoming traffic.
</p><p>In Europe, headlamps approved for use on one side of the road must be adaptable to produce adequate illumination with controlled glare for temporarily driving on the other side of the road,<sup id="cite_ref-R112_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R112-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>p.13 ¶5.8</span></sup>. This may be achieved by affixing masking strips or prismatic lenses to a part of the lens or by moving all or part of the headlamp optic so all or part of the beam is shifted or the asymmetrical portion is occluded.<sup id="cite_ref-R112_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R112-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>p.13 ¶5.8.1</span></sup> Some varieties of the <a href="/wiki/Headlamp#Projector_(polyellipsoidal)_lamps" title="Headlamp">projector-type headlamp</a> can be fully adjusted to produce a proper LHT <i>or</i> RHT beam by shifting a lever or other movable element in or on the lamp assembly.<sup id="cite_ref-R112_77-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R112-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>p.12 ¶5.4</span></sup> Some vehicles adjust the headlamps automatically when the car's <a href="/wiki/GPS" class="mw-redirect" title="GPS">GPS</a> detects that the vehicle has moved from LHT to RHT and vice versa.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rear_fog_lamps">Rear fog lamps</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Left-_and_right-hand_traffic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Rear fog lamps">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the European Union, vehicles must be equipped with one or two red <a href="/wiki/Automotive_Lighting#Rear_fog_lamps" class="mw-redirect" title="Automotive Lighting">rear fog lamps</a>. A single rear fog lamp must be located between the vehicle's longitudinal centreline and the outer extent of the driver's side of the vehicle.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crash_testing_differences">Crash testing differences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Left-_and_right-hand_traffic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Crash testing differences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An Australian news source reports that some RHD cars imported to that country did not perform as well on crash tests as the LHD versions, although the cause is unknown, and may be due to differences in testing methodology.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rail_traffic">Rail traffic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Left-_and_right-hand_traffic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Rail traffic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rail_handedness_by_country.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Rail_handedness_by_country.svg/220px-Rail_handedness_by_country.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="112" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Rail_handedness_by_country.svg/330px-Rail_handedness_by_country.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Rail_handedness_by_country.svg/440px-Rail_handedness_by_country.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2754" data-file-height="1398" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rail_handedness_by_country.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #cc0000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Trains use right-hand track</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #204a87; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Trains use left-hand track</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #d3d3d3; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Rail traffic is mixed or lacking</div></div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Double-track_railway" title="Double-track railway">Double-track railway</a></div>
<p>In most countries, rail traffic travels on the same side as road traffic. However, in many cases railways were built, often using LHT British technology, and road traffic switched to RHT while rail remained LHT. Examples include: Argentina, Belgium, Bolivia, Cambodia, Chile, Egypt, France, Iraq, Israel, Italy, Laos, Monaco, Myanmar, Nigeria, Peru, Portugal, Senegal, Slovenia, Sweden, Switzerland, Taiwan, Tunisia, Venezuela, and Yemen. In Indonesia it is the reverse (RHT for rails (even for LRT systems) and LHT for roads). France is mainly LHT for trains, except for the classic lines in <a href="/wiki/Alsace-Lorraine" title="Alsace-Lorraine">Alsace-Lorraine</a><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> which belonged to Germany when the railways were built before 1918. China is basically LHT for long-distance trains and RHT for metro systems. Spain, which is RHT for railways has LHT for metros in Madrid and Bilbao. Metros and light rail sides of operation vary, and might not match railways or roads in their country. Trams generally operate at the same side as a road traffic due to a common sections with roads.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Worldwide_distribution_by_country">Worldwide distribution by country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Left-_and_right-hand_traffic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Worldwide distribution by country">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Driving_standards_historic.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Driving_standards_historic.png/220px-Driving_standards_historic.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="102" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Driving_standards_historic.png/330px-Driving_standards_historic.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Driving_standards_historic.png/440px-Driving_standards_historic.png 2x" data-file-width="1357" data-file-height="628" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Driving_standards_historic.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Countries with left- and right-hand traffic, currently and formerly. Changes since 1858 when <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> changed to the right are taken into account.<br /><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #b41624; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;RHT</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #E10000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Now RHT, formerly LHT</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #2c2edc; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;LHT</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #7DAEFF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Now LHT, formerly RHT</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ab12bc; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Formerly a mix of LHT and RHT in various parts of the country, now RHT</div></div></div></div>
<p>Of the 195 countries currently recognised by the United Nations, 141 use RHT and 54 use LHT on roads in general. A country and its territories and dependencies are counted as one. Whichever directionality is listed first is the type that is used in general in the traffic category.
</p>

The <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands" title="Channel Islands">Channel Islands</a> (Jersey and Guernsey) drove on the right under <a href="/wiki/German_occupation_of_the_Channel_Islands" title="German occupation of the Channel Islands">German occupation</a> lasting from 1940 to 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>

			<div class="thumb" style="width: 193.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ecke_K%C3%A4rntnerstra%C3%9Fe_Annagasse_mit_Werbetafel_Tabarin_und_Chapeau_Rouge_ca_1930.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Ecke_K%C3%A4rntnerstra%C3%9Fe_Annagasse_mit_Werbetafel_Tabarin_und_Chapeau_Rouge_ca_1930.jpg/290px-Ecke_K%C3%A4rntnerstra%C3%9Fe_Annagasse_mit_Werbetafel_Tabarin_und_Chapeau_Rouge_ca_1930.jpg" decoding="async" width="194" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Ecke_K%C3%A4rntnerstra%C3%9Fe_Annagasse_mit_Werbetafel_Tabarin_und_Chapeau_Rouge_ca_1930.jpg/434px-Ecke_K%C3%A4rntnerstra%C3%9Fe_Annagasse_mit_Werbetafel_Tabarin_und_Chapeau_Rouge_ca_1930.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Ecke_K%C3%A4rntnerstra%C3%9Fe_Annagasse_mit_Werbetafel_Tabarin_und_Chapeau_Rouge_ca_1930.jpg/579px-Ecke_K%C3%A4rntnerstra%C3%9Fe_Annagasse_mit_Werbetafel_Tabarin_und_Chapeau_Rouge_ca_1930.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2218" data-file-height="1380" /></a></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-croatiaslavonia-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-croatiaslavonia_12-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news">Baedeker, Karl (1900). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/01703017.5423.emory.edu">"Austria, including Hungary, Transylvania, Dalmatia and Bosnia"</a>. p.&#160;xiii–xiv<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">28 July</span> 2017</span>. <q>In Styria, Upper and Lower Austria, Salzburg, Carniola, Croatia, and Hungary we keep to the left, and pass to the right in overtaking; in Carinthia, Tyrol, and the Austrian Littoral (Adriatic coast: Trieste, Gorizia and Gradisca, Istria and Dalmatia) we keep to the right and overtake to the left. Troops on the march always keep to the right side of the road, so in whatever part of the Empire you meet them, keep to the left.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.atitle=Austria%2C+including+Hungary%2C+Transylvania%2C+Dalmatia+and+Bosnia&amp;rft.pages=xiii-xiv&amp;rft.date=1900&amp;rft.aulast=Baedeker&amp;rft.aufirst=Karl&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2F01703017.5423.emory.edu&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALeft-+and+right-hand+traffic" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
